In these cases, it was not possible to identify in the SR customs database corresponding to imports to 6 JCDs with a statistical value of CZK 692,800.
Of which:
- to 2 export JCDs from the customs database of the Czech Republic with a statistical value of CZK 293,024, the corresponding import JCD could not be identified in the SR customs database (see paragraphs 6 and 7).
Due to a certain error rate in entering data into the customs database, it was not possible to identify some registered imports, e.g. in cases where the name of the importer was missing or there was an error in the tax identification number (hereinafter referred to as the "DIČ").
It is therefore not possible to conclude with certainty that those exports did not materialise.
- for 4 export JCDs from the customs database of the Czech Republic, imports corresponding to exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic with a statistical value of 399 776 CZK have not been demonstrated (see paragraph 5).
In such cases, suspicion of a fictitious export of goods for the purpose of unauthorised application of the VAT deduction is justified.
5. Control of imports and exports of goods and services as part of the control of selected financial or tax authorities
5.1. Control sample
In order to verify the fulfilment of the obligations of tax entities in the import and export of goods and services, and to evaluate the level of cooperation between the financial and customs authorities, the files of the tax authorities were selected on the audited financial or tax authorities according to the following criteria:
1. tax entities were selected from the customs databases of the Czech Republic and Slovakia: (a) with the highest volume of imports, (b) with the highest amount of VAT charged, (c) with the highest volume of exports,
2. from the registration of financial or tax authorities were selected tax entities according to the above: (a) applied VAT deductions on importation, (b) excess VAT deductions applied, (c) entitlement to a refund of SpD, (d) the VAT exemption claimed on export,
3. tax entities for which tax control has been carried out.
Sampling was made regardless of the country of destination with a focus on risky commodities.
5.2. Control findings in the Czech Republic
An overview of imports into the Czech Republic and exports from the Czech Republic for risky commodities in 2002 and 2003 is shown in the following table:
5.2.1. Check of the selected sample at the tax authorities
The SAO focused mainly on the audited tax authorities:
- the procedure of the tax authorities in verifying the correctness of the declared VAT,
- the cooperation of the tax authorities with the customs administration,
- comparing the data from the customs database with the JCD database at the tax authorities with a focus on comparing the declared value, applying the correct VAT rate following the customs classification of goods or services, or comparing the data on the JCD and the accompanying invoices with the particulars in the customs database,
- Comparison of VAT return data on transactions made and received from abroad with data in the JCD database at the tax authorities or in the customs database,
- how to verify the right to VAT exemption when exporting goods or services abroad,
- a comparison of the volume of exports of goods or services declared in VAT tax returns with data from the customs database.
The JCD database at the tax authorities contained only records of those imports and exports for which tax entities were required to include imports or exports in the tax return.
The audited tax authorities examined the files of the tax entities, which reported in VAT returns in 2002 and 2003 imports totalling CZK 60,396,379,000 and the corresponding VAT totalling CZK 12,644,052.
Furthermore, the files of the tax entities reporting in tax returns for the years 2002 and 2003 were checked with exports totalling CZK 47,323,111,000.
According to the Czech Republic's customs database, tax entities for which imports and exports were examined between the Czech Republic and Slovakia, carried out imports into the Czech Republic from Slovakia in 2002 and 2003 with a total VAT base of CZK 3,759,352,000, VAT amounting to CZK 767,607,000 and exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic with a total statistical value of CZK 1,989,090,000.
The identified differences between the records in the JCD database at the tax authorities and in the customs database of the Czech Republic were examined as follows:
- JCD items that were missing from the customs database of the Czech Republic were requested directly at the General Directorate of Customs of the Czech Republic,
- for JCD items missing from the database of tax authorities, it has been verified that these are not cancelled documents,
- for JCD items for which different values were found between the customs database of the Czech Republic and the database of the tax authorities, the relevant JCDs were requested at the General Directorate of Customs of the Czech Republic in written form, including the attached invoices.
The screening revealed that the JCD database at the tax authorities did not include, for example, records of import JCDs with a total VAT value of CZK 3,622,000 and records of export JCDs with a total statistical value of CZK 9,359,000.
5.2.1.1. Detected differences
By comparing the JCD database at the tax authorities and the cumulated tax liability for 2002 and 2003 in the VAT returns for the selected sample of tax entities, the following differences were found:
Differences in values were mainly due to:
- The time shift between the realisation of imports and the exercise of the right to deduct VAT charged on the importation of goods.
The taxpayer was entitled to deduct the tax on the importation of goods within 3 years of the end of the tax year during which the goods were placed under the relevant scheme or, in the case of the temporary admission procedure, no later than 3 years after the end of the tax period during which the scheme was terminated.
- The time difference between the realisation of exports and the moment when the taxpayer received JCD.
The taxpayer was obliged to provide data on realized exports exempt from the obligation to apply output tax to the VAT return at the earliest for the tax period in which the goods came out of the country if they simultaneously had a tax document, which was JCD.
- Failure to comply with the obligations of tax entities when reporting imports and exports in VAT returns.
In some cases, tax entities did not include correct data on imports and exports in VAT returns.
Since tax entities did not have to attach JCD lists to the VAT return filed, it has been made more difficult to control the right to deduct VAT or VAT exemption by the tax authorities.
5.2.2.2. Synergies between the financial and customs authorities
During the audited period, following a decision by the customs authorities, import or export duties and VAT were refunded or remitted.
Tax entities were required by law to reduce the previously exercised right to deduct VAT by the amount of tax returned by the customs office, in a tax return for the period in which the tax was returned.
No legislation provided for the transmission of information on changes in the amount of customs duties and VAT from customs offices to the tax authorities.
Some audited tax authorities received this information, but irregularly or with considerable delay and only from some customs offices.
One audited tax office did not receive this information at all.
If the tax office did not receive information on changes in the amount of duty and VAT directly from the customs office, it did not have the information necessary to check compliance with the legal obligation of tax entities.
In 2002, customs authorities refunded VAT of more than EUR 319 million. CZK and in 2003 in the amount of over 398 mil. CZK.
The results of the audit pointed to persistent shortcomings in the activities of tax authorities, some systemic errors in the cooperation of the tax authorities with the customs authorities and the failure of certain obligations of VAT and SpD payers.
In the administration of VAT and SpD, weaknesses were identified in particular in the following areas:
(a) on the abolition of VAT registrations, the fulfilment of the obligation on payers to pay VAT on the registered assets, or which should have been registered on the date of cancellation of the registration in respect of which the payer applied the deduction, verified only rarely,
(b) the tax authorities have not consistently used the possibility of carrying out a tax audit or local investigation.
In this case, in some cases, there was a lack of verification of the right to deduct VAT,
(c) the official records and reports on the oral hearing did not contain all the necessary data demonstrating the legitimacy of the excess VAT deductions applied,
(d) the tax authorities did not sufficiently verify the imports and exports reported in VAT returns.
5.3. Control findings in SR
5.3.1. Check of selected sample at tax offices
For the selected sample of the SAO SR, it verified or the tax office carried out a tax audit for the tax years 2002 and 2003.
After analysing the filed tax returns and refund requests from selected tax entities of the SAO SR, the tax office asked for the provision of the relevant JCDs documenting imports and exports, invoices and proofs of payment of the tax.
Subsequently, the SAO of the Slovak Republic checked that the submitted JCD was registered in the customs database of imports and exports and the fulfilment of decisive facts and legal conditions when applying the deduction of input tax by way of importation, when the excess VAT deduction was refunded, the application of the exemption in respect of exports and the refund of SpD.
In 10 audited tax offices, the SAO of the Slovak Republic verified selected cases of imports and exports of goods between the Slovak Republic in the structure according to the respect of JCD items and the sum of the statistical values given in the following table.
In total, imports were checked at a statistical value of CZK 856.2 million. Sk, which represents 5.11% of the total value of imports of selected commodities from the Czech Republic into SROV.
Exports of selected commodities were checked at a total statistical value of 176 million. Sk, which from the total value of exports of selected commodities from the Slovak Republic to the Czech Republic represents 6.87%.
In addition, 618 cases of imports of additional goods and services (mainly white technology, packaging and software) from EU countries were checked into the Slovak Republic with a total statistical value of CZK 532.8 million. Sk.
5.3.2. Synergies between tax and customs authorities
The SAO OF THE SR's audit found discrepancies between the data registered in the SR customs database and the data reported in specific VAT returns, e.g.:
- 2 tax entities (natural persons) reported in tax returns imports of goods with a total value of 1 589 261 Sk, on the basis of which they claimed a refund of excess VAT deductions.
In local investigations carried out at the instigation of the SAO OF THE SR, tax entities were unable to prove to the tax authority the legitimacy of the application of input tax with the relevant documents on the implementation of imports and the payment of VAT,
- In the tax return for the January 2003 tax year, the taxpayer declared the taxable transaction received at the value of 77 167 576 Sk, the export of goods worth 18 006 041 Sk and claimed an excess VAT deduction of 17 157 101 Sk.
VAT returns filed for 2003 show that the difference between the volume of purchases and exports of 59 161 535 Sk, which arose in January 2003, was not settled throughout 2003.
In the above cases, the SAO SR asked the relevant tax authorities to carry out a tax audit of the excess VAT deductions applied.
- in another case, the tax entity (s.r.o.) for the January to June 2002 tax year declared in VAT returns the export of goods with a total value of 87 199 Sk.
By verification in the SR customs database, it was found that the taxpayer made exports at a statistical value of 296 747 Sk during that period.
The tax office carried out a local investigation multiple times at the company's headquarters, finding that the company's managing director (UK citizen) had travelled and the company had left the leased premises without terminating the lease in August 2002.
At the time of the SAO's audit of the SR SAO, the tax administrator filed a complaint with the law enforcement authorities on suspicion of committing the crime of non-taxation.
Based on the results of the audit at selected tax offices, the SAO of the SR notes that the biggest problems in the performance of the administration and control of VAT and SpD occurred mainly in the following areas:
- non-use of the possibility of carrying out VAT control on problematic tax entities, in particular in cases of high value of excess VAT deductions;
- the tax inspection protocols did not contain an inventory of the documents audited
- JCD on the import and export of goods,
- insufficient screening of reported imports and exports of goods in tax returns,
- lengthy management of tax administrators in issuing decisions, in particular in the context of the assessment procedure, even as a result of the termination of the right to impose penalties;
- non-compliance with legal conditions when granting tax deferrals;
- non-compensation of VAT arrears within the deadline for the refund of excess VAT deduction,
- insufficient use of all forms of security and recovery of tax arrears.
On the basis of the SAO's examination of the findings, the SR asked the relevant tax authorities to carry out a VAT check with a focus on the legitimacy of the excess VAT deductions applied for 14 tax entities.
Measures have been taken at all audited tax offices to remedy the identified deficiencies, the implementation of which will be monitored and monitored by the SAO of the SR.
6. Conclusion
International cooperation has made it possible to examine trade transactions carried out between trading partners from both states and pointed to shortcomings in the import and export records.
The results of the checks also identified persistent shortcomings in the fulfilment of VAT and SpD obligations, in the activities of financial or tax authorities, in the system of cooperation and exchange of information between tax authorities and customs administrations, which ultimately reduced the efficiency and effectiveness of tax controls.
The lessons learned also showed that the tax authorities in the Czech Republic have basic data on imports and exports from the Czech customs database within the tax information system. There is no similar system for transmitting information on imports and exports of goods between customs and tax administrations in the SR.
The procedure for verifying the performance of the VAT and SpD management was not the same, given the different rules and conditions in the territory of the two States.
The SAO verified the necessary data from information obtained from the customs administration of the Czech Republic and from the documents available at the audited tax offices. The SAO of SR verified the execution of commercial transactions from documents of tax entities requested through tax authorities.
If irregularities were found, he asked the tax authorities to carry out tax inspections.
The parallel VAT and SpD checks carried out assessed the state of administration of these taxes in the period prior to joining the European Union and pointed to the need for further cooperation in this area.
Mutual cooperation will further deepen in the control of the activities of financial or tax authorities in connection with the adoption of the new VAT Act.
One of the new obligations related to the delivery of goods to other Member States of the European Union is the submission of a so-called recapitulative statement.
This report must be made by all VAT payers who have made the supply of goods to another Member State to a person registered for VAT in another Member State.
Tax administrators are required to enter data from recapitulative statements into the VIES (VAT Information Exchange System) to which tax administrations from the Member States of the European Union have access.
Financial or tax authorities then have the possibility to compare these data with the data contained in the filed VAT returns.
This system is further used to verify the validity of the VAT number of persons registered for VAT, to verify the validity of the VAT exemption applied on the supply of goods to another Member State and to check the proper taxation of goods.
The implementation of parallel control actions showed the possibilities of cooperation between the SAO and the SAO SR.
The results of these control actions will be confronted with the outputs of upcoming control actions, which will, among other things, focus on examining the VIES system, which is intended to prevent tax evasion in so-called "intra-community supplies."
Given the importance of VAT and SpD for budget revenues, control action needs to be focused primarily on monitoring and identifying risky areas in order to limit the scope for tax evasion to indirect taxes.
Communications
This report was prepared in Czech, English and Slovak and signed by the Vice-President of the Supreme Audit Office and the chairman of Najýššie's control department of the Slovak Republic.
Report on the results of the inspection at asylum facilities in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic
State property and funds earmarked for securing the activities of the Administration of Refugee Facilities of the Ministry of the Interior
Audit Action No 04/23 "Property of the State and funds earmarked for securing the activities of the Administration of Refugee Facilities of the Ministry of the Interior"
Audit Action No 15/2004 "Control of pubs with the assets of the staff and financial prostheses intended for the provision of asylum and temporary attacking cudzincom in SR"
1. Introduction
Population migration is one of the phenomena that has a global character and has become an inseparable part of the current development stage of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic.
Its cause is, in particular, the flight of citizens from their country of origin or last residency from persecution, in particular for reasons of racial, religious, national, political, as well as the consequences of military conflicts and ethnic cleansing.
Their aim is to gain asylum and integrate into society, especially in some of Europe's developed democratic countries.
A large group is also made up of foreigners, whose reason for migration is unfavourable economic and living conditions.
Migration is not a new phenomenon.
The setting of concrete principles for aid to refugees has already been brought about by the adoption of the 1951 Geneva Agreement and the 1967 New York Protocol on the status of refugees, the acceptance of which the governments of the signatory countries have pledged to apply them in their foreign-policy orientation.
The Czech and Slovak Federative Republic ratified the New York Protocol in 1991 and the Geneva Agreement in 1992.
After the split in 1993, both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic declared their position on the refugee issue by continuing to implement adopted international documents and confirmed their binding nature within the framework of national legislation by a legal act.
Both republics have also enshrined the granting of asylum in their constitutional laws.
The asylum process and related issues were targeted parallel checks carried out by the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Najýš Control Office of the Slovak Republic, the results of which are contained in a joint final report.
Cooperation between the highest control authorities of both countries was implemented on the basis of the "Contract of Cooperation in Control Activities" (hereinafter referred to as the Treaty") concluded in April 2004.
The two Parties undertook to provide each other with information in the Treaty on the legislation governing the asylum procedure in the State of the other Contracting Party, selected statistical data on the number of asylum seekers and the results of the asylum process, as well as information on asylum facilities.
The aim of the parallel control action was to set out the same problem areas and to highlight the consequences of this for the area of population migration.
Cooperation consisted in the harmonisation of the control action programme, the exchange of information to the extent provided for by the Treaty and the drawing up of a joint final report on the outcome of the checks.
The first joint meeting, which took place from 5.4. to 7.4.2004, set out the same headings of the audited issue and control procedures for the preparation of control actions.
At the second meeting, which took place on 28.6. to 30.6.2004, the common points for the control action programme were specified.
During the third joint meeting, which took place during the audit operation on 8.11. to 10.11.2004, there was an exchange of experience from the ongoing inspections.
The forms of outputs from the checks were specified and the structure and content of the joint final report drawn up from 14.2. to 17.2. 2005 at the last joint meeting were set out.
The checks were carried out in parallel between September 2004 and January 2005.
In the Czech Republic, the inspection was carried out by auditors of the Department of Defence and Security and selected territorial unions.
The audited bodies were the Ministry of the Interior (hereinafter referred to as "MV") and the Administration of Refugee Facilities of the Ministry of the Interior (hereinafter referred to as "SUZ").
In the Slovak Republic, the defence section, vnútra and the staff of the úradov carried out a check.
The audited entity was the Migration Department of the Ministry of Slovakia (hereinafter referred to as "MÚ").
The audited period was 2001, 2002, 2003 (the period checked).
In some cases, statistical data over a longer period of time are also used in the joint report.
2. Recapitulation of control results
2.1. 2.1. Assessment of control activity
The subject of parallel checks was: - asylum process, - asylum facilities, - management of funds and state property, - internal control system.
The mutual exchange of experience gained between the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Najýš Control Office of the Slovak Republic took place at the stage of preparation and implementation of control actions.
This exchange made it possible to evaluate the supporting documents submitted more thoroughly, to analyse the causes of the identified differences in the safeguarding of the asylum process.
This made it possible to focus on the decisive problems affecting the activities of asylum facilities.
Ultimately, this positively affected the quality of carrying out the control activity.
2.2 Summary of the results of the inspection
The harmonisation of the asylum law of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic with European Union documents (EU) has been the subject of evaluation reports by the European Commission on the readiness of both countries to join the EU.
The adoption of legislation governing this area continues after the accession of both countries to the EU in order to further align with EU law.
The number of asylum seekers in the Czech Republic increased significantly in 2001.
In the Slovak Republic, the number of applicants has a steadily growing trend, with a significant increase in applicants in 2001.
Within the meaning of international agreements, the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic provide financial and material security to asylum seekers.
The state budget of both countries earmarks the necessary funds for this purpose.
The amount of expenditure drawn from the state budget of both countries is influenced in particular by the number of applicants, the duration of the asylum procedure until granting or not granting asylum, as well as the health of applicants.
The amount of some selected benefits is comparable in both countries.
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic set up asylum facilities in which they gradually created suitable conditions for the stay of participants in the asylum procedure.
During the stay, asylum seekers are provided with accommodation, catering, health care and basic material equipment and sanitary items.
At the same time, courses are organised as part of leisure activities and applicants are created the conditions for cultural and sporting activities.
Asylum facilities include reception centres and residential centres, in the Czech Republic as well as integration centres.
The cost of one residential day depends on the occupancy and equipment of the Centre and is comparable in both countries.
In the Czech Republic, the capacity of asylum facilities is basically stabilised.
In the Slovak Republic, it is projected to increase by almost 1,000 seats in the next two years.
The aim is to create the conditions for applicants, whose growing number is expected to continue in the coming years in the context of the application of the Dublin Convention.
In the Czech Republic, the highest part of the approved budget expenditure was spent on securing services. The decisive expenditure of this item was the expenditure on the accommodation and catering of applicants in asylum facilities operated under the contract, the healthcare expenditure provided to asylum seekers and the costs of a permanent security service ensuring the protection of asylum facilities.
A significant part of the total expenditure was also spent on salaries, payments for work carried out and premiums.
In the Slovak Republic, the budgetary management of the security of migration policy and the asylum process was marked by many budgetary measures that addressed legitimate requirements related to the increase in the number of asylum seekers.
Current expenditure was a decisive factor in the execution of current expenditure on wages, salaries and premiums, expenditure on materials and services and expenditure on security services.
The results of the audit in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic highlighted some similar shortcomings that did not have a major impact on the financial management and management of the state's property.
These were shortcomings, in particular, in the area of the use of state budget funds in breach of the purpose for which they were determined by the State Budget Act, when ordinary expenditure was covered by actions having the character of a technical appreciation of fixed tangible assets.
The Accounting Act in the field of inventory and non-compliance with the accounting curriculum was also violated.
In the Czech Republic, minor deficiencies were also found in the disposal of state property when it was left to use another person without a contract.
Failure to comply with the deadline for reporting changes to the Land Registry data violated the Land Registry Act and leaving the waste for disposal to a person who was not a person authorized for waste management was violated by the Waste Act.
In the Slovak Republic, shortcomings were also found in the provision of disproportionately high advances for electricity and natural gas abstraction, shortcomings in public procurement and the management of state property when it was not sufficiently secured by its registration and the method of liquidation of unnecessary assets was not proven.
The internal control system has been secured in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic within the meaning of the generally binding legislation in force governing the way financial control is carried out.
No deficiencies were found in the written documentation submitted.
Despite some identified shortcomings, the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and Najvyšší control úrad of the Slovak Republic, evaluate positively the procedure of SUZ and the Municipality in the provision of services for asylum seekers.
Based on the lessons learned during the inspection, the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Najvyšší Control Office of the Slovak Republic note that despite a significant increase in the number of asylum seekers in recent years, the relevant organisational units of both countries have managed tasks related to the care of asylum seekers at an appropriate level.
The evaluation shows that the system is functional and stable.
The results of the inspection were discussed with interested statutory representatives of the respective organizational units of the state on the Czech and Slovak sides.
Based on the results of the audit, we recommend that the positive methodological experience of the audit be used in other joint control actions in the framework of international cooperation of supreme audit institutions.
3. Legislative framework
In both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, asylum law is based on the 1951 Geneva Convention (Convention on the Status of Refugees and the Secondary New York Protocol of 1967).
The first legislation was implemented by Act No. 498/1990 Coll., on Refugees.
Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum and on Amendments to Act No. 83/1991 Coll., on the Police of the Czech Republic, has been effective in the Czech Republic since 1 January 2000.
This Act has been amended a total of five times (Act No. 2/2002 Coll., Act No. 217/2002 Coll., Act No. 320/2002 Coll., Act No. 519/2002 Coll. and Act No. 222/2003 Coll.).
Two other amendments to Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, are currently in the legislative process, and the adoption of further amendments will be tied to European legislation.
At the time of the creation of Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, the area of asylum was part of the 3rd pillar of the EU and asylum documents were in terms of binding political documents.
Already in the context of the adoption of this law, there was an effort to make it as compatible as possible with EU law.
A major change for asylum policy within the EU was the transfer of asylum issues from the 3rd to the 1st pillar, when the right of asylum became part of Community law from 1.5. 1999 on the basis of the Treaty of Amsterdam.
This shift has created the need to create adequate legislation, i.e. to create legal instruments in appropriate legal binding nature.
The compatibility of Czech asylum law with EU documents has been the subject of regular evaluation reports by the European Commission.
Key Community regulations in the field of asylum were adopted shortly before or after the Czech Republic joined the EU and their transposition period at the time of the audit of the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic has not yet expired.
After their implementation into the Czech legal order, the Czech Republic will be obliged to notify the European Commission of how it carried out the transposition.
In the Slovak Republic, the procedure of the state authorities in the procedure for granting refugee status and the determination of the obligations of foreigners who applied for refugee status or who were granted temporary refuge in the territory of the Slovak Republic was established with effect from 1.1.1996 by the National Council of the Slovak Republic No 283/1995 Z. z., on the refugees.
Since 2003, the Refugee Act has been replaced by Law No 480/2002 Z. z" on asylum and amending and supplementing certain laws incorporating the harmonisation of the legislation of the Slovak Republic with Directive 2001/55/EC of the Council of Europe of 20.7.2001 on minimum standards for granting temporary protection in the event of a mass influx of displaced persons and on measures to promote the balance of efforts between Member States in the adoption of these persons and the endure of the resulting consequences.
After two amendments to Act No 480/2002 Z., on Asylum, in 2003 and 2004, a third amendment was approved at the beginning of 2005, which means further harmonisation of the Slovak Republic's law with EU legislation on migration and asylum.
Its important elements are, for example, the establishment of an obligation for asylum seekers to attend a course of the foundations of the Slovak language for the duration of their stay at the integration centre, the designation of a guardian by the courts of non-funded foreigners, if they are staying on the territory of the Slovak Republic without a legal representative, allowing applicants to enter into an employment relationship under certain circumstances mentioned in the amendment to the Act.
The harmonisation of the Slovak Republic's law with EU law on migration and the asylum process has been assessed in regular European Commission reports on the readiness for the Slovak Republic's accession to the EU.
In 2002, the report highlighted the lack of staffing capacity of the Ministry of the Ministry, which did not correspond to the increased number of asylum seekers.
4. Asylum Process
In the Czech Republic, since 1990, matters relating to refugees fall within the competence of the MV.
With effect from 1 January 1996, the MV has established an organisational unit of the state of SUZ. This was due to the state administration's exercise department in the field of asylum procedures, which is carried out through the Department of Asylum and Migration Policy of the MoI, from the provision of services to asylum seekers.
The task of SUZ is to provide accommodation, catering, health and other services to participants in asylum and asylum procedures for the MV.
In the Slovak Republic, it performs decisive tasks in the area of the IHR's asylum process, in particular in terms of financial and material-technical security of the asylum process to the extent defined by specific regulations.
Priority tasks are issues related to the decision-making of foreigners' applications for asylum at level I of the administrative procedure and the comprehensive operation of asylum facilities in the areas of accommodation, catering, health, financial and material security for asylum seekers.
In addition, it is preparing concepts for the state's migration policy.
The results achieved in the area of the creation of the asylum system in the Slovak Republic in recent years have been significantly influenced by cooperation and support from the Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR).
A special benefit was his financial contribution to the reconstruction of the housing fund for asylum seekers.
The UNHCR has also co-financed social, cultural and sporting activities for applicants.
4.1 Development of the number of asylum seekers and the results of the asylum process
The evolution of the number of asylum seekers in the Czech Republic is shown in the graph.
A significant increase occurred in 2001 due to the Kosovo crisis and an increased number of refugees from Chechnya became apparent in 2003.
An overview of the results of the asylum procedure in the Czech Republic is shown in Table 1.
A total of 394 asylum were granted in the audited period, 83 of them in 2001, 103 in 2002 and 208 in 2003.
In the audited period, no asylum was granted in a total of 22,731 cases and in 22,636 cases the asylum procedure was terminated.
The number of parties to 1.1. includes both persons who have not yet been subject to a decision and those to whom the decision has not been referred.
There are also included persons who have been waived to miss the time limit for lodging an appeal, or who have been annulled the previous decision and have not yet been issued a new one.
In the Slovak Republic, the number of asylum seekers has a steadily growing trend.
If 96 applicants were registered in 1993, it was already 10,358 in 2003.
A significant increase in the number of applicants occurred in 2001, when 8,131 applicants were registered, an increase of 423% compared to 2000, in which 1,556 people applied for asylum.
Data on the results of the asylum procedure in the Slovak Republic in the audited period are shown in Table 2.
A total of 49 asylum-seekers were granted in the audited period and the number did not exceed 0.22% of the registered number of applicants in each year.
Asylum was not awarded in 860 cases.
The asylum procedure was discontinued in a total of 23,088 cases.
The bulk of applicants, by staying outside the asylum facility without permission, voluntarily left the asylum procedure before it was completed.
In 2002, it was more than 82% of the total number of registered applicants, and by more than 94% in 2003.
The duration of one applicant's stay at one asylum facility, converted into an average number of days, was 13 days in 2001, 18 days in 2002 and 19 days in 2003.
The findings of the Ministry also show that for most applicants entering the asylum process in the Slovak Republic, it is about finding better living conditions, i.e. so-called economic migration, which has no basis in documents adopted by the international community, to which the Slovak Republic has also signed up.
In these cases, the asylum system is purposefully used by the applicant.
An application for asylum based on economic reasons, such as seeking employment or better living conditions, is no reason to grant asylum.
Such an application should be considered as manifestly unfounded under the provisions of Section 12 of Article 12(a) of Act No 480/2002 Z.
Internationally recognised principles for the identification of economic migrants are not yet established to distinguish them from applicants persecuted in their country of origin and that would allow the asylum process to be accelerated.
In view of the above, it can be concluded that the Slovak Republic is, in terms of international migration, transit countries and destination countries have been and remain Western European states.
After joining the EU, the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic acceded to the Agreement determining the State responsible for examining asylum applications lodged in one of the Member States of the European Communities (Dublin Convention).
The Convention prevents multiple purpose applications for asylum in several EU countries.
Applicants are returning to the country of first registration after positive identification in EURODAC.
The Slovak Republic is part of the outdoor border of EU countries, therefore the application of the Dublin Convention may in the following period result in an increase in the number of applicants who will need to create the conditions for residence in the Slovak Republic until the decision to grant or not grant asylum.
4.2 Drawing of funds
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic are obliged, within the meaning of international agreements and the national legislative arrangements based on them, to provide financial and material security to asylum seekers.
The state budget of both countries earmarks the necessary funds for this purpose.
The amount of expenditure drawn from the state budget is mainly influenced by the number of applicants, the duration of the asylum procedure until the final decision on the granting of asylum, as well as their state of health.
The growing number of people entering the asylum process has a direct impact on the higher use of statutory benefits for applicants such as food, pocket money, expenditure on basic material equipment, other material equipment and other social contributions.
The amount of some selected doses is comparable in both countries.
In the Czech Republic, in the audited period, there was a meal allowance of CZK 58 per person per day, an allowance of CZK 12 per person per day and CZK 6 per child under the age of 15.
The cost per asylum seeker per residential day was on average CZK 351 in 2001, CZK 383 in 2002 and CZK 345 in 2003.
In addition to pocket money, asylum seekers staying in residential centres with separate cooking and applicants staying in private outside asylum facilities may receive a contribution up to the minimum subsistence level.
Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, as amended, admits, among other things, in § 84 municipalities, to a contribution to the reimbursement of costs incurred in connection with the asylum facility on its territory.
The amount of the contribution was determined annually by government resolutions and in the audited period amounted to CZK 7 per person per stay day.
At the same time, municipalities are entitled to a contribution to the non-investment costs of primary schools attended by asylum seekers.
The amount of the allowance was CZK 120 per pupil in the audited period and a month, provided that in a given month the person attended the school for a minimum of 10 days.
In the Slovak Republic, in the controlled period, there was a meal allowance of 70 Sk per person per day, pocket money 12 Sk per person per day and 8 Sk per child under the age of 15.
The cost per asylum seeker per residing day was on average 442 Sk in 2001, 447 Sk in 2002 and 445 Sk in 2003.
The municipalities on whose territory the asylum facility is located is granted, in accordance with Law No 480/2002 Z. z., on asylum, a partial reimbursement of expenses incurred by the municipality in connection with the establishment and activities of an asylum facility.
So far, municipalities have only partially benefited from the possibility of applying for a contribution, which is set at 1 500 Sk per bed and calendar year.
National legislation in both countries for issuing a decision on asylum sets a 90-day period from the start of the asylum procedure and at the same time allows for an extension of this deadline in justified cases.
The most common reason for extending the asylum procedure, which affects the amount of state budget expenditure, is the submission of remedies against the decision not to grant asylum.
In the Czech Republic, the highest number of final decisions was made between 90 and 180 days (31.54%).
The second highest number was within 90 days of the date of the application for asylum (17.61%).
In total, 73% of asylum procedures initiated in 2001-2003 were finally decided within one year of their initiation.
Within two years of the start of the asylum procedure, 92% of asylum applications were finally decided.
In the Slovak Republic, according to MÚ data, up to 90% of applicants who have not been granted asylum use the statutory possibility of extending the time limit by filing an appeal to the court.
In some cases it was an extension of more than a year and at the time of the audit, the proceedings were not closed in court.
Some contribution to the shortening of the asylum procedure is expected from the establishment of joint workplaces of the Municipality and Úrad Border and cudzineckej polície Policajný zboru in the reception centres in Adamov-Gbeloch, Opatovskej Novej Vsi and in Liptovské Vlachoch, which was approached at the time of the inspection from 1.10.2004.
This envisages simplifying procedures and shortening procedural deadlines between the two components of the asylum process.
5. Asylum devices
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic set up asylum facilities in which they gradually created suitable conditions for the provision of services to the participants in the asylum procedure.
During the stay, asylum seekers are provided with accommodation, meals, health care, pocket money and basic material equipment and sanitary items during their stay.
At the same time, courses are organised as part of leisure activities and applicants are created the conditions for cultural and sporting activities.
5.1 Asylum equipment in the Czech Republic
SUZ manages and operates asylum facilities and is responsible to manage the state's property in the premises of the Vyšní Lhoty reception centre, the reception and residential centre Červený Újezd, the residential centres Bělá-Jezová, Zastávka u Brna, Kostelec nad Orlicí, Havířov and in the premises of the integration asylum centres Jaroměř and Zastávka u Brna.
At the beginning of 2002, a reception centre was established in the transit area of Prague-Ruzyně Airport.
SUZ also operates asylum facilities in Stráž pod Ralskem, Seč, Bruntál, Zbýšov and Kashava, which are operated by a legal entity on the basis of Section 80 (3) of Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum.
The total accommodation capacity of the reception centres is 747 seats and the total accommodation capacity of the residential centres is 1,977 places.
Admission centres are closed establishments which may not be allowed to leave asylum seekers until the prescribed medical examinations and basic admissions listed in Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum.
Violations of the obligation to stay at the reception centre are assessed as a misdemeanor.
The residency centres to which asylum seekers from reception centres come are used to house asylum seekers until the decision on their application for asylum or not to grant asylum becomes final.
Subject to certain conditions, asylum seekers have the opportunity to leave or stay in private.
Applicants for asylum, in reception and residential centres in which they are reported to reside, have the right to free accommodation, the provision of meals, other services and pocket money, provided that they have tolerated the acts necessary to ascertain their financial and property circumstances, and those circumstances are such that even partial reimbursement of those services cannot be fairly claimed from them.
Integration asylum centres are used for temporary accommodation for persons who have been granted asylum.
Clients are accommodated in the resort on the basis of an accommodation contract, which is usually concluded for 6 months with the possibility of an extension until adequate housing can be provided for them, or they will secure it themselves as part of the state integration program.
In the integration centre, clients pay for all accommodation costs and eat individually.
Persons with the recognised status of a refugee have the same rights and obligations as Czech citizens and have the same access to social security.
5.1.1 Costs of asylum facilities
To compare asylum facilities, he was chosen as the best-sensifying indicator of the cost of one physical residential day.
It is based on the actual number of persons to whom the services provided by the asylum facility were actually provided every day.
Table 3 and in the graph are devices operated by SUZ designated as own and facilities operated by legal persons on the basis of Section 80 of Act No.325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, as contractual.
The cost of one residential day in its own asylum facility operated by SUZ decreases with increasing fulfilment of these facilities. This is due to the fact that the fixed costs of running the premises are budgeted for more asylum seekers.
The volume of total costs incurred to provide services for asylum seekers increases proportionately with their increasing numbers.
In 2001 and 2003, applicants in crisis capacity were accommodated in their own asylum facilities.
In part of 2003, emergency accommodation capacity in residential containers, caused by the mass arrival of asylum seekers from Chechen nationality from Poland, even used extraordinary accommodation capacity in residential containers (a total of 320 beds).
This capacity was secured by renting 80 residential and 6 sanitary containers.
Spending per applicant per day of residence was then lower in those years.
However, long-term maintenance of the high filling of these devices is not possible.
Account should be taken of the risks of an unexpected influx of refugees and the associated requirement for the need for higher accommodation capacity.
At the same time, it is not possible to carry out some repairs, maintenance and investments with fully occupied accommodation capacity.
The form of asylum facilities operated contractually allows, where necessary, the adoption of operational solutions in the area of the capacity of asylum facilities.
5.2 Asylum facilities in the Slovak Republic
Asylum facilities are reception centres and residential centres.
The Municipality has in its administration the reception centres Adamov-Gbely, Rohovce, Opatovská Nová Ves and the residential centre Brezová pod Bradlom.
The total capacity of the reception centres is 470 seats and the residential centre is 100 seats.
The growing number of asylum seekers was dealt with by the Municipality by setting up a residential centre in Gabčíkov in the rented premises of the Slovak Technical University of Bratislava with a flexible disposable capacity from 250 to 940 seats.
According to the contract concluded, the leaseholder also ensures the provision of services related to the operation of the centre.
In this form, the Ministry dealt with the lack of its own staff.
The residential centre was also temporarily established in 2002 and 2003 in leased premises in Jazierciach with a capacity of 160 seats.
Admission centres had an average occupancy rate of 57% to 62% in the audited period, with an average of 33% to 50% in residential centres.
Occupancy changed according to the season and the highest was in the last months of the year, when many more applicants applied for asylum compared to other months of the year.
Further increase of accommodation capacity by about 450 seats will be possible after the reconstruction of the resorts in Opatovskej Novej Vsi and Vlachoch.
Following the completion of the Humennom Centre in 2006, the number of seats will increase by another 550 people.
5.2.1 Cost of asylum facilities
Data on the cost of running asylum facilities are shown in Table 4.
Their analysis shows that the operation of an asylum facility by another body providing premises and ensuring the operation of the facility, including the related activities, is one of the options to ensure, at reasonable costs, the tasks arising from the stay of asylum seekers in the Slovak Republic.
6. Management of funds and state property
6.1 Economic analysis
In the Czech Republic, in 2001, approved budgeted SUZ expenditure was increased by 13 budget measures by CZK 103,077,000.
Of this, 89,979 thousand CZK (87.29%) were purpose-designed funds. The bulk of it was intended to address the consequences of the Kosovo crisis (CZK 44,538,000) and the provision of health care provided to asylum seekers (CZK 38,870,000).
In 2002, SUZ spending was increased by 20 budget measures by CZK 88,362,000.
Of this, CZK 62,282,000 (70.48%) were earmarked funds, the bulk of which (CZK 23,200,000) was earmarked for health care provided to asylum seekers.
In 2003, SUZ spending was increased by fourteen budget measures by a total of CZK 88,078,000.
Of this, 82,000 thousand CZK (93.09%) were earmarked funds released from the General Treasury Administration.
The amount of CZK 51,000,000 was earmarked for health care provided to asylum seekers, CZK 24,000,000 to address the influx of Chechen refugees and CZK 7,000,000 for a program of humanitarian evacuations of disabled residents of the Iraqi Republic.
An overview of the drawdown of selected SUZ expenditure in the audited period is given in Table 5.
It is clear from the above overview that the highest part of the expenditure was spent on providing services.
The amount of expenses spent on the purchase of services ranged from 37.07% to 38.23% of total expenditure.
The outrageous expenditure under this item was the costs of accommodation and catering of applicants in asylum facilities operated on the basis of a contract, medical treatment expenses provided to asylum seekers and the costs of a permanent security service providing security in individual asylum facilities.
A significant contribution to SUZ's total expenditure was spent on salaries, payments for work and premiums (23.99% - 24.97%).
Last but not least, total SUZ expenditure was significantly accounted for by material purchases (around 14%), with food purchases accounting for the highest share of these expenditures (7.61% ? 9.83% of total expenditure).
In the Slovak Republic, the MÚ's budgetary economy to secure migration policy and the asylum process has been marked by more budgetary measures that addressed legitimate requirements related to the increase in the number of asylum seekers.
In particular, in 2001 and 2002, the draft and breakdown of the budget in the expenditure part were characterised by an imbalance between the requirements of the Ministry, which were based on an increase in the number of asylum seekers and the financing needs for their legal claims, and the resources provided mainly in the main category of current expenditure.
The administrator of the budget chapter disproportion dealt with several budgetary measures over the course of the year, increasing the current expenditure limit by 41.8% in 2001 and up to 71.7% in 2002.
An overview of the drawdown of selected MÚ expenditure in the audited period is shown in Table 6.
The implementation of current expenditure was a decisive factor in spending on wages, salaries and premiums ranging from 24.47% to 30.51% and expenditure on materials and services ranging from 21.04% to 38.82% of total expenditure.
More than 10% were spending on security services and from 3.92% to 7.20% on food purchases.
Another significant part was spending on accommodation and catering services by a share of 10.82% to 26.96%.
The relatively low share in the range from 1.03% to 6.25% was capital expenditure.
6.2 Shortcomings identified
The results of the audit in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic pointed to some similar shortcomings that did not have a major impact on the management of state funds and property.
These were shortcomings in particular in the following areas: (a) the use of state budget funds in violation of the purpose for which they were determined by the State Budget Act when ordinary expenditure was covered by actions having the character of a technical appreciation of fixed tangible assets; (b) a violation of the Accounting Act in the field of inventories and in non-compliance with the exchange chart.
In the Czech Republic, minor deficiencies were also found in the disposal of state property, when it was left for use by another person without a proper contract; failure to comply with the deadline for reporting changes to land registry data violated the Land Registry Act; leaving waste for disposal to a person who was not a person authorized to manage waste was violated by the Waste Act.
In the Slovak Republic, shortcomings were also found in the provision of disproportionately high advances for electricity and natural gas abstraction, shortcomings in public procurement and the management of state property when it was not sufficiently secured by its registration and the method of liquidation of unnecessary assets was not proven.
7. Internal control system
In the Czech Republic, an internal control system was introduced for SUZ on 1.7.2002, within the meaning of the provisions of Act No. 320/2001 Coll., on Financial Control, determining the competences of individual employees responsible for carrying out preliminary, ongoing and subsequent control.
At the same time, an internal audit system was put in place and an internal SUZ auditor was appointed.
Every year, SUZ is issued a plan of financial, socially and operationally technical inspections aimed at comprehensive verification of the management of state funds and property in individual asylum facilities managed by SUZ.
In 2002, 42 management checks were carried out and 54 were carried out in 2003.
In the Slovak Republic, the direct exercise of control activities in the departments and organizations of the Ministry of the Interior of the Slovak Republic in terms of compliance with generally binding legislation and internal regulations, the Úrad ensures the Minister's checks.
The audited period did not check the performance of tasks in the asylum policy section.
The internal control system of the MÚ is focused primarily on the implementation of preliminary and ongoing financial control under Act No 502/2001 Z. z., on financial control and vnútornom audite.
The performance of the financial control is also regulated by the internal regulations of the Director of the Ministry of Industry.
The substantive focus of the control activity as part of the management of individual departments was established within the framework of the annual plan of the Ministry of Industry.
Responsible for the exercise of control in this area were identified by trade union directors.
According to the written documentation submitted, deficiencies were not identified.
Since the Regulation of the Ministry of the Interior of the Slovak Republic No 28 of 23.4.2004, the Ministry of the Ministry of the Slovak Republic has been obliged to handle and investigate complaints under the Complaints Act.
In the near term, the organisational security of this task will need to be resolved.
Communications
This report was prepared in Czech, English and Slovak and signed by the Vice-President of the Supreme Audit Office and the chairman of Najýššie's control department of the Slovak Republic.
Joint report on parallel expenditure controls on the construction of the motorway Prague – Dresden
Introductory word
Without the existence of trans-European transport networks, a modern, dynamic and, above all, united Europe would be difficult to imagine.
Quality transport networks are a prerequisite for maintaining and raising the standard of living of Europeans.
Creating conditions for fast, safe, reliable and cheap transport of people and costs is an important task for the state administration.
It is a very demanding task both in organizational and technical terms and financially.
European efforts at sustainable mobility often and hard to find compromises with Europe's interest in maintaining a healthy and human-friendly environment.
Construction of transport infrastructure, also thanks to the necessary measures in favour of the environment, are financially challenging.
Considerated procedures and decisions of the state administration in the preparation and acquisition of transport constructions can bring significant savings from public financial resources that can be used differently for the benefit of citizens.
One of the roles of the audit institutions is to actively seek out shortcomings that have led to less than achievable cost-effectiveness, efficiency and effectiveness of expenditure on transport infrastructure construction.
The audit institutions should not only alert the state administration to the shortcomings identified, but recommend measures that lead to an improvement in the situation, for the benefit of the citizens, for the benefit of Europe.
Short summary
The two highest audit institutions of the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic (hereinafter referred to as Germany and the Czech Republic) were carried out by the Bundesrechnungshof (BRH) and the Supreme Audit Office (SAO) in 2005 and 2006 parallel inspections of expenditure on the construction of the motorway Prague – Dresden (distance D 8 in the Czech Republic and motorway A 17 in Germany).
The highways are connected by a border bridge co-financed by both countries.
Thanks to cooperation with the SAO, BRH was able to carry out a thorough check on the efficiency of the funds spent on the construction of the border bridge.
On the basis of close cooperation with BRH, the SAO obtained the necessary data on the amount of German bridge and tunnel costs for comparative purposes.
The Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany concluded a contract for the construction of a border bridge in 2000.
According to the construction process, the completion date was eventually scheduled for the end of 2006.
During the award of the contract, however, the Road and Motorway Directorate of the Czech Republic (hereinafter referred to as the RMD) made mistakes that led to an extension of the duration of the competition and the reduction of the construction time.
The contract with the contractor was thus signed later than anticipated.
The contractor agreed to build the bridge in a shorter time and ensure its completion in 2006.
As a result, however, the RMD had to accept a significant increase in the price of the bridge.
As part of the joint inspection work, the prices of bridges on motorways D 8 and A 17 were compared on the basis of the conversion into m2 of the bridge structure area.
The statistical analysis of prices made it possible to identify bridges with high costs per m2 area.
Analysis revealed that the border bridge was the most expensive bridge in a group of large bridges with a length of over 100 metres.
The price increase could also be attributed to the delay that occurred during the procurement procedure.
When analysing the cost of building the tunnels, inspectors found that the prices of tunnels on the D 8 motorway were significantly higher than those of comparable tunnels in Germany.
BRH and the SAO now have a database of the prices of bridges and tunnels, on which they will be able to rely on for future checks on the effectiveness of the means used for transport infrastructure purposes.
For the construction of bridges, both control institutions recommend: standardization of bridge structures and the use of coupled steel-concrete and special structures only in cases where prestressed reinforced concrete bridges are not adequate.
One of the main hallmarks of the collaboration was her team spirit and informality.
During the parallel control, the auditors were involved in their specific knowledge and skills.
Introduction
This report reports on the progress and results of parallel checks on funds spent on the construction of the motorway connecting Dresden and Prague (D 8 in the Czech Republic and A 17 in Germany).
The checks focused on the economy, efficiency and effectiveness of the expenditure incurred.
Parallel checks were carried out by BRH in Germany and the SAO in the Czech Republic.
Parallel checks took place in 2005 and 2006.
The SAO audited the economy, efficiency and effectiveness of spending of approximately CZK 20 billion including VAT (approximately €690 million) for the border section of the D 8 motorway, which has the figure 0807.
Of this amount, €64 million from European funds (ISPA) was planned to be used.
In addition to the parallel control carried out jointly with the SAO, BRH controlled the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
The funds spent on the construction of all three sections of the A 17 motorway amounted to around €670 million, of which €277 million will be co-financed by European funds (EFRE).
The highway from Prague to Dresden is part of the Trans-European Transport Corridor IV. Dresden – central and southern parts of Eastern Europe – Istanbul.
The D 8 motorway runs from Prague through Ústí nad Labem to the state border.
Highway A 17 runs from the state border of the Czech Republic/Germany to Dresden.
At the time of the inspection, border sections of both motorways were under construction.
These sections were put into operation on 21 December 2006.
On the German side, the motorway is completed all the way to Dresden, while it is not finished on the Czech side of the D 8 motorway, because the section no. 0805 of 16.4 km through the Bohemian Central Mountains between Řehlovice and Lovosice is still in the preparatory phase.
According to the plan, section No 0805 between Prague and Ústí nad Labem is to pass through the protected landscape area of the Czech Central Mountains.
According to forecasts made in the Czech Republic in 2001, there should be an operating intensity of 22,940 vehicles per day in 2008 on the border section No 0807 of the D 8 motorway.
According to German forecasts, the traffic intensity at the Czech border will total about 25,000 vehicles per day in 2010.
In 2000, Germany and the Czech Republic concluded the Treaty between the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic on the interconnection of the German motorway A 17 and the Czech motorway D 8 on the common state border by building a border bridge.
Both states are involved in the construction of the bridge, so the inspection of the construction of this bridge was an important aspect of the parallel checks carried out by the two inspection institutions.
BRH and SAO cooperation
Since mid-2005, the two highest audit institutions have discussed close cooperation in controlling the construction of the motorway connecting Prague and Dresden.
The two sides had serious reasons for such cooperation:
1.
Germany is financially involved in the construction of the border bridge.
Its planning and construction rests with the RMD.
Therefore, BRH itself did not have the opportunity to carry out a check of this construction project.
2.
The SAO did not have the comparative database needed to assess whether the funds were used economically in terms of the overall level of costs for bridges and tunnels on D 8.
3.
The SAO needed to ensure the effectiveness of the expenditure, taking into account the main technical parameters of the motorway, in particular the arrangement of bridges and tunnels, which should allow the same quality of the journey on both sides of the border.
Following preparatory negotiations, the presidents of the two supreme audit institutions were signed on 4 January 2006 by the Coordinated Control Agreement on the D 8/A 17 motorway.
The joint working meetings of the BRH and SAO inspectors took place throughout 2006 at an interval of approximately six to eight weeks.
Current problems and tasks were addressed at the meetings, in particular the preparation of a uniform methodology for checking the prices of bridges and tunnels, the exchange of findings from the control of the border bridge construction and the preparation of joint reports.
An important sign of cooperation was the ongoing exchange of information.
While BRH carried out most of the work on comparing the technical parameters of bridges and tunnels and tender procedures, the SAO largely prepared a methodology for controlling the prices of bridges and tunnels.
The applicable legal rules for government control differ in both countries in the way control actions are closed.
For these reasons, the two highest audit institutions drew their own control conclusions on their findings, obtained during their inspections.
This report reflects the results achieved in parallel checks.
The cooperation will be completed by signing the final communiqué in the first half of 2007.
Construction of the border bridge
In the 2000 treaty, the Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany agreed on a date for the completion of the construction of the border bridge, as far as possible, in 2005.
The two sides then agreed to postpone this deadline until the end of 2006.
The RMD was active as an investor.
Germany is financially involved in the construction of the bridge from 42.13%.
In the period up to 1999, the RMD proposed that the border bridge should have a prestressed reinforced concrete structure.
In 1999, in agreement with its partners in Germany, the RMD agreed to a coupled steel-concrete structure.
That decision was justified by a reduction in the number of double pillars from seven to five and the subsequent environmental benefits of such a solution to the valley.
In addition, the comparative calculations carried out by the RMD stated that the coupled steel-concrete structure should be a less expensive option.
But there was a mistake in the calculations.
They stated that the new coupled steel-conconcrete structure would be only 397 m long.
In fact, it's 5% longer.
During the award of the contract for the border bridge, the RMD made serious errors.
It has not prepared a custom dossier that adequately describes the assessment criteria and sub-criteria for comparing tenders.
This error led to the evaluation and evaluation of tenders and the Director-General evaluating the bids in a subjective and problematic manner.
The unsuccessful tenderer challenged the results of the contract procedure.
A subsequent investigation by the Office for the Protection of Competition resulted in the contract for the award of the construction contract being signed later than originally envisaged.
This meant that the 2004 summer construction season, after evaluating the bids, was wasted and the construction time was reduced.
The construction company agreed to build the bridge in a shorter period of time, but at the cost of a substantial increase in costs.
The offered procedure included a double assembly of the bridge superstructure, a change in the structure of the pillars and the establishment and equipment of the assembly space.
In addition, the construction company was unable to procure the originally designed type of steel within the necessary time.
The new type of steel – at a higher price by about €4 million, which had to be fully paid by the RMD – allowed for a reduction in the time to perform anti-corrosion protection, which in turn made it possible to reduce the overall construction time of the border bridge.
Compared to the net price of €18.3 million offered in the tender, the total cost of building the border bridge will increase considerably as a result of the accelerated procedure and other adjustments in funding.
The reduced time earmarked for construction work has led to a price increase of about €5.9 million.
Currently, the net cost amount is around €25.9 million, without taking into account automatic price increases (as of October 2006).
Comparison of prices of bridges and tunnels
The two highest control institutions carried out an analysis of the prices of bridges on motorways D 8 and A 17 in order to identify bridges whose price was too high and to compare the prices of bridges on the German and Czech sides.
The analysis was based on methods of mathematical statistics.
Furthermore, tunnels on the D 8 were compared with similar tunnels on motorways in Germany (A 71, A 38 and A 4).
There are no similar tunnels on the A 17 motorway.
In preparing this part of the check, it was important to accurately identify the relevant prices.
The two highest control institutions set the following principles for comparison of bridge prices: based on the actual price paid for the bridge without value added tax (VAT rates vary in the Czech Republic and SNR and are subject to change); disregard prices for special bridge foundations; rewrite prices in CZK at the rate of the European Central Bank valid as at 30 December 2005 (CZK 29 = € 1).
On both D 8 and A 17, there are bridges of various structures (steelo-concrete, prestressed reinforced concrete, coupled steel-concrete and steel) and various sizes.
In an effort to ensure the homogeneity of the structures studied, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of structure.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyses.
By far the most common type of construction on both sides of the border were concrete bridges (20 bridges on D 8, 36 bridges on A 17).
The analysis focused primarily on this group of bridges, as the number of the set allowed the use of mathematical-statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of the results.
Checks found that prices per m 2 concrete bridges on both D 8 and A 17 depend on the size of the bridge (its length, area): the larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of the bridge structure.
For German bridges, the price dependence on size was stronger than for Czech bridges (see charts below).
Two concrete bridges on the A 17 and four on the D 8 have been identified, the price of which is significantly higher.
A method was used to locate these bridges to detect atypical values in a set of numbers and a regression analysis taking into account the dependence of the price of the bridge on its size (for the determination of atypically expensive bridges, the standard distance from the regression curve upwards was used).
However, information on the atypically high price of these bridges should only be seen as an indicator on which bridges need to be targeted.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the reasons for the high price should be analysed in each identified case.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative measures, or environmental protection requirements or any other acceptable reason.
In view of the fact that Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than the German ones (see table below), the proven negative dependence of the price of the bridge on its length could lead to the assumption that Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The lower prices of Czech concrete bridges are also supported by lower labour costs in the construction sector in the Czech Republic compared to Germany.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges on the D 8 are higher than on the A 17.
During the joint negotiations, two possible causes of this situation were identified:
1.
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional firms to participate in the competition for the contract.
In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of a contract for part of the motorway route, which is several kilometres long.
Small and medium-sized companies do not participate in such a competition because the contract is too big for them.
2.
There is greater variability in bridge structures in the Czech Republic than in Germany.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of bridge structure was decided long before its construction.
The decision shall be taken at the time when the RMD is submitting an application for a zoning decision.
The RMD at this time selects types of bridges from multiple variants, but cost-effectiveness plays a minor role in decision-making.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could only be reached on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction work market in both countries, which would, however, go beyond this control action.
The border bridge is the most expensive coupled steel-concrete bridge on D 8 and A 17.
In the case of tunnels, it was found that the prices of the tunnels on the D 8 were twice as high as the tunnels in Germany.
Comparing the main construction-technical parameters of the tunnels on the D 8 with the tunnels in Germany, however, showed no significant difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the field of tunnel construction in the Czech Republic.
However, the small number of tunnels did not allow generally applicable conclusions to be drawn.
Effectiveness and efficiency of the construction of the Dresden motorway – Prague
The independent Czech Republic was established in 1993.
In the same year, the government approved the concept of developing the motorway network.
The D 8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the state border with Germany.
Section 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains was to be taken before the border section no. 0807.
The Czech Republic is acquiring the D 8 motorway as part of the programme financing.
In programme No 327 220 "Construction of the D 8 motorway Prague – Ústí nad Labem – state border of the Czech Republic/SRN" set two targets for the construction of the D 8 motorway:
1.
improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem – national objective;
2.
improve the connection between Prague and Berlin within the IV Trans-European Transport Corridor Berlin-Istanbul - international target.
According to the program, the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains was to be completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section No 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added here that the completion date is not identical to the date of commissioning.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains is now at the stage of an official zoning decision.
Environmental activists challenged that decision with legal actions.
The actions are not finally decided.
It cannot therefore be excluded that the section running through the Czech Central Mountains will be completed a few years later than stated in the programme documentation.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed postponing the completion date of section No 0805 to 2010.
As a result of these facts, the construction of the border section did not achieve a national goal – improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The RMD made an estimate of capital expenditure for the border section D 8 No 0807 and section No 0805 via the Czech Central Mountains in 1999-2001.
The estimate for this capital expenditure of the included investment costs is about €639 million combined for both sections 0805 and 0807.
Table 2: Economic assessment results (EIRR = internal rate of return)
All the highways and expressways under consideration opened, both sections of the D 8 opened in 2007.
