On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 25,000 vehicles per day in 2010
The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and section through the Czech Central Mountains would be completed in time.
At the time of the inspection, however, it is clear that the section through the Czech Central Mountains will not be opened at the original date.
This will reduce the above EIRR values.
The cost of both sections excluding VAT, calculated as at 31 December 2005, was € 897 million, or 27,4 % higher than the cost level with which the 2001 economic analysis worked.
But there is a risk that section 0805 will be conducted in a different, more costly route, which would affect the aforementioned line of economic viability.
Economic analysis showed that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable at least 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but a study addressing the issue of itineraries for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains envisages an intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, pushing the investment below the economic viability threshold.
With a higher load of itineraries for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains, there is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which were not foreseen in the original economic analysis.
The aforementioned reduction in revenues and cost increases means that the already low EIRR that the construction of the border section D 8 was not to be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
Also in the construction of the A 17 motorway, there was a similar cost overrucy as in the case of these two sections on D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the federal road development plan for long-distance transport for 1992, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany envisaged a cost of about €320 million.
The resulting cost-to-income ratio was 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have soared to about €670 million, and the cost-benefit ratio has more than halved.
In addition to the parallel control carried out jointly with the SAO, the BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
In doing so, BRH found that the competent approval state authority had requested an off-road tunnel for Section A-17, which was not needed.
During the inspection, BRH managed to convince the Federal Ministry and the German Road Construction Administration that the tunnel was not necessary.
The result was savings, which amounted to roughly €10 million and €0.15 million in annual operating costs alone.
When assessing the first Section A 17 BRH, BRH found that the construction project was not properly prepared.
This is illustrated by the following example:
At the preparatory stage, the Road Construction Administration did not investigate the conditions of the bedrock in the rocks of Dölzschen because the site was difficult to access.
This was done only after the tunnel stamping machine hit a rock.
Tests showed that the rocks inside were more weathered than anticipated during the selection process.
Therefore, costly measures such as anchoring, injection and protective net against stone falling had to be taken, which required about €3.3 million.
The Roads and Motorways Directorate had to bear the costs not only of other emergency operations, such as security measures in the rocks of Dölzschen, but also for measures that would not have been needed if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes the costs associated with the interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), further training of drilling workers (€0.5 million) and rising wages and expenditure on goods as a result of exceeding the duration of the project (€7 million).
The cost overruns thus amounted to around €10.7 million, which was not matched by any added value.
Following the lessons learned from these and other audited projects, BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter management in its oversight of road construction work.
The Federal Ministry should give the Road and Motorway Directorate the necessary incentive to implement quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and SAO Cooperation fulfilled the intention of both audit institutions.
BRH gained control knowledge of the preparation, assignment and course of the construction of the border bridge.
The two highest control institutions have built a database of the prices of bridges and tunnels, on which future controls will be able to rely.
The SAO found that the main construction-technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the route of the D 8 motorway are no different from the A 17 parameters.
The inspectors of the two institutions involved in the cooperation have gained new experience, which they can use for further inspections.

Coordinated control of funds earmarked for financing projects carried out under the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013
INTRODUCTION
The emerging community of the people of Europe is the driving force behind cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
So let this community give the opening statement on this joint report by the two highest control institutions of neighbouring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral territories, often remnants of darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help mitigate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers, and to further facilitate the development of the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: some cover coastal areas, other inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most of the programs are bilateral, but some include more than two neighbouring countries."
The programmes last for seven years and a total of EUR 5.7 billion is earmarked for them from the EU budget.
"The programmes include the following activities: repair and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycling trails and bridges, investments in cross-border waste management systems, health facilities, research centres, flood control measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or the development of tourist destinations, development of common services for the local population, advice on employment issues, the creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the programme is provided on condition that organisations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to implement projects based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly and decide which projects to be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself has ended.
The funds shall be provided on condition that the project partners from both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four characteristics: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint funding."
Within the framework of the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, funding and control of the activities of the project are clearly defined and distributed to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership include: joint choice of management partner, joint project preparation and application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, assuming overall responsibility for the implementation of the project by the lead partner, the overall support payments are accepted by the lead partner and carry out their transfer to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects carried out on the territory of the regions and voivodeships adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme of Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007-2013 (OPPS ČR-POL).
The implementation of the operational programme was allocated €258,187,467.
The total sum included a contribution from European funds (ERDF) totalling €219,459,344 (85%), which must be exhausted by the end of 2015.
Financial data on the use of EU funds from the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation The Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013 at the end of 2013 is presented in the following table:
The agreement on cooperation between the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the presidents of both offices on 27 February 2013 following previous mutual negotiations at the meetings of the heads of the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia, held in Lovasberény, Hungary, in September 2012.
The aim of the cooperation was to examine whether the implementation of the operational programme is carried out in accordance with the legislation and with binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system operates reliably, whether the objectives of the operational programme are being achieved and that the selected projects are implemented in accordance with the specified conditions of the support provided.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel check, in which the control authorities, on the basis of their statutory mandate, independently examined topics the content of which had been jointly discussed in advance.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The inspection carried out also contained elements of a performance audit.
Selected topics were examined on a sample of projects for project partners on both sides of the border.
The results of the checks carried out were the audit findings published in the usual manner by both authorities.
On the basis of the two control conclusions set out in the Annex, this Joint Report was drawn up.
The joint control sample selected included six projects (Annex 1) which fall within the area of aid 1.1 - Strengthening transport accessibility and support area 2.2 - Promoting the development of tourism.
Prior to the start of the audit, the absorption of funds from these two areas of support accounted for more than 60% of the funds reimbursed to beneficiaries by the end of 2012 for all projects carried out throughout the OPPS CR-POL.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to controlling a joint sample of six projects, the SAO audited another 13 projects and NIK two others (all in the area of support 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to the beneficiaries of the ERDF aid, which were audited by the SAO and the NIK, totalled EUR 25 362 174 (SAO) and EUR 8 450 784 (NIK).
The audited volume of appropriations represented 41 % of all funds paid to beneficiaries of aid from aid areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONCLUSION CONCLUSIONS
Both inspections independently carried out by the SAO on the Czech side and NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of irregularities identified did not exceed the level of significance.
The implementation of the OPPS ČR-POL, including the functioning of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The audited persons who were part of the management and control system have taken steps to limit the risk of misuse of funds and the non-use of ERDF funds provided.
Evidence of the effectiveness of the implemented projects under the operational programme is a high level of achievement of the objectives set, which was also supported by the results of the survey of the societal evaluation of project implementation.
SUMMARY OF RESULTS OF CONTROLS
A. Evaluation of the control and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to carry out a check of the management and control system of the OPPS ČR-POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry for Regional Development of the Czech Republic), the National Coordinator (Ministry for Regional Development POL) and Controllers (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodeship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit made it possible to evaluate the tasks and functions carried out by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on the activities carried out in connection with the setting and performance of the function of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in the context of setting the objectives of the programme and monitoring of the progress achieved, evaluation activities and annual reports, assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, the absorption of the programme funds and the fulfilment of the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on subsidies, as well as the control activities of the managing authority, setting up the performance of activities and outputs from audits of the entrusted audit entity and with the system of reporting and monitoring of irregularities and its compliance, at the same time, the coordination of tasks and cooperation between the institutions involved in the CRL.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and the drawing up of project implementation control plans were examined and the evaluation of project applications carried out by independent evaluators was assessed.
The examination of the control system included a review of administrative and on-the-spot checks, which allowed the approval of, for example, the supply of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, while the compliance of the disbursement of these funds and related activities (or parts thereof) with EU national and EU legislation.
Significant deficiencies were identified by the SAO's audit in the area of publication of correct, complete and up-to-date information on the subsidies provided.
In the area of evaluation and approval of projects, the risk of non-transparency of this process has been identified.
Other deficiencies were identified in the area of setting up monitoring indicators and setting up the irregularity reporting system.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and control system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
The irregularities identified by the NEV relating to the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to violations of existing laws and rules have not been assessed as significant.
These discrepancies had an impact only on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the entire OPPS CR-POL.
Despite the identified discrepancies, NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The audit did not show a contradiction between the indicators and the objectives set for each project and those defined by the operational programme.
The expected results were achieved in all projects audited, the funded activities were carried out and the indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the project applications of the operational programme were met.
The audit conclusions are in line with the result of the survey of the societal evaluation of the implementation of the projects described below in the text.
An important aspect that the SAO and the NIK have paid attention to in their conclusions is the limited possibility of objective evaluation of the fulfilment of the projected results of individual projects with regard to the objectives of individual projects and at the same time the objectives of the whole operational programme.
At the initiative of the Supreme Audit Chamber, a poll conducted in the summer of 2013 at the site of the implementation of projects selected for the joint control sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nysa (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the investigation was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both with the public in Poland in relation to a part of the project carried out by Polish project partners and with the Czech public in relation to a part of the project carried out in the Czech Republic.
The survey mainly concerned the appropriateness, quality, exchange of information, use of funds and the societal function of projects, including investments made.
The number of respondents for individual projects is given in Annex 2.
The assessment of the responses from the survey indicated that the overall societal evaluation of the projects was good and reached 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), meaning that the results of the projects were positively perceived by the majority of respondents both with regard to the objectives and other parameters mentioned in the project applications.
According to respondents, the projects examined fulfilled the criteria expressed in the operational programme.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourism infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and restoring contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of the responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of achievement of the objectives/parameters (actual impact rate) and the level of the expected respondents.
This difference applies to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the views of participants in panel discussions (target groups) carried out under the NIK control on the Polish side, who pointed to the need for project implementation and confirmed their positive public acceptance and the fulfilment of expected results.
C. Evaluation of the legality of the projects audited
For the beneficiaries of the aid, the compliance of the project with the timetable and content of the project application was examined, the fulfilment of the stated objectives and purpose of the project, the fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, the management of changes in projects, the handling of any project revenue, the fulfilment of the obligation of separate accounting records of project transactions, compliance with the ban on the financing of projects from other EU sources, the implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions, the fulfilment of mandatory publicity concerning in particular the dissemination of awareness of the sources of financing of projects from the EU, compliance with the eligibility conditions of project expenditure, the collection of suppliers and the verification of deliverables on the basis of public contracts awarded.
The total financial value of shortcomings by qualified SAO and NIK as ineligible for EU funding was estimated at €85 094.
However, this amount did not exceed the specified level of materiality relating to the total controlled volume of appropriations for the sample of projects.
Despite the discrepancies found, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of selected projects positively by the beneficiaries of the aid.
In most cases, the commitments resulting from the conditions of the aid were duly fulfilled by the beneficiary.
The funds earmarked for the implementation of the audited projects were spent in accordance with EU and national law regulations.
Nevertheless, seven of the eight beneficiaries audited by NEVs were found to have irregularities in the implementation of in-kind and financial obligations.
These were individual cases of non-systemic nature and concerned, in particular, inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breach of public procurement rules, which did not have an impact on the choice of supplier or the supply of contractually agreed works.
Only in one of all NIK-controlled projects can the irregularity found, relating to non-compliance with reporting obligations, be assessed as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which €8,231 financed by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure of CZK 2 098 053 in three of the 19 audited projects (of which €76,863 financed by the ERDF).
Irregularities included non-delivery of paid material and work or expenditure for the modernisation of a property owned by a person who had no link to the project either as a beneficiary of the aid or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATIONS
The joint recommendation of the SAO and the NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system in particular with regard to the consistent setting of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational programme.
In general, efforts must also be made to identify such indicators at both programme and project level, which will allow for a more reliable verification of the implementation of the identified impacts.
Notes and recommendations resulting from the checks carried out on both sides of the border are included in the separate control conclusions of the SAO and the NIK on CDs.
COMMUNICATION
The report was prepared in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the Presidents of the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Procurement of public works contracts and the prevention of corruption
Joint Parallel Control Report by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
Public procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and private spheres.
The role of the state is to use public resources as efficiently and economically as possible and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor in the success of the Single Economic Area.
In its rules, the EU has set limits whereby the contracting authority must comply with certain requirements to guarantee a broad and unlimited competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public procurement that are values below EU limits, regulation of their procurement remains within the remit of individual countries.
Moreover, public procurement is prone to corruption with the consequent high potential for damage.
Therefore, contracting entities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and incorrect favouritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI - SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI - BRH) agreed to carry out parallel checks on procurement procedures and the prevention of corruption in land and transport constructions.
Cooperation was also focused on the area of European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
The checks included procurement below European limits in order to penalise the area of corruption prevention.
The audit findings of the two supreme audit institutions are summarised in a joint report, which is addressed, inter alia, to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Control areas and audited institutions
The BRH assessment is based on blanket checks of the aforementioned areas carried out on German building administrations responsible for federal transport and land constructions between 2011 and 2012.
For example, highway construction and large-scale building projects were evaluated.
BRH included findings from past inspections in the evaluation.
The SAO evaluation is based on inspections carried out during the years 2008 to 2012, which focused on construction of roads, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other buildings in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, the award of federal public contracts is mostly carried out by the provincial construction administrations.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement in the area of transport infrastructure is the responsibility of state institutions for road and railway construction.
In the field of building construction, public procurement is carried out by beneficiaries of budget funds.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used at the various stages of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation must take into account differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and subordinate standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany are governed by budgetary law and the relevant administrative acts .
Internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details on the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called federal government tender manuals, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail with regard to legal precedents in the area of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation relating to contracts with value above and below European limits.
The terms of reference were made to substantial changes in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened the transparency of procurement procedures, opened up room for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption during the procurement procedure.
Basic aspects of procurement law in both countries
As regards European procurement procedures, open and narrower procedures in particular need to be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European limit are awarded in accordance with national regulations (public call for competition, limited call for participation, negotiated procedure), but these are virtually identical to the above conditions for the European procurement procedure.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates on a European scale are invited) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and when calling for competition, any bidder may submit an offer.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized firms.
This is achieved primarily by splitting the overall public contract into commercially specific parts and dividing into sub-contracts.
The award of contracts divided into these parts gives the opportunity to participate in the proceedings as many candidates as possible.
The two characteristics of German procurement law are time-tested instruments for obtaining good value for money.
In Germany, more than 330,000 construction companies are registered with 1.9 million employees.
In the Czech Republic, the conditions of European procedures must be applied even in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
The exception is a simplified sub-prime procedure where the contracting authority can use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
The current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between open management and closer management.
85,000 construction companies are registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
Total number of contracting authorities for public works and share of individual types of procedure in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction work in Germany amounted to 7 675 billion.
Of which CZK 1,138 billion (15%) was the share of public contracts, which, in addition to contracts awarded by the federal government, included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities.
The total volume of projects of the federal road and earthworks (without the construction of railways and water constructions) amounted to CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of federal contracts for land works and transport works was as follows: 80.7% open procedures and public calls; 12.7% closer management and narrower calls;
6.6% of the negotiated procedure and direct procurement; 33.2% is the share of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic in 2011 reached CZK 118.6 billion , which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009, it reached CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total.
Structure of procurement types in 2011: 65.1% open procedure; 14.3% narrower procedure; 9.5% negotiated procedure; 8.4% simplified procedures for sub-prime contracts; 2.7% other procedures.
The figures include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
These figures show that: parallel controls in both countries have been targeted at a substantial part of public procurement procedures; the prevention of corruption, which must still be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open relevance. procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of the main results
The SAO and BRH carried out parallel controls on the award of European works contracts, with an emphasis on the prevention of corruption in their countries.
The checks focused on comparing the legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public procurement in the field of land and transport construction.
The key findings are also provided:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national rights of both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
Preventing corruption is not only the aim of the EU directive, but of the national procurement rules of the two countries, which differ in part in terms of focus and in detail.
In both countries, the essential requirement to comply with the tender documents is the inclusion of all the information needed to prepare tenders.
The specification shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements restricting competition.
The tender documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the problem is the lack of definition of the subject of the contract.
In Germany, a division of contracts is required into parts where it is only possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to BRH's findings, this requirement is respected in Germany.
The SAO's inspections showed that significant heavy public works in the field of road construction are commissioned in the Czech Republic without being divided into parts or divided into parts, but only into a very significant volume. This method can distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
These limitations on market potential can result in significant cost increases and give rise to the risk of non-serious price negotiations and inadmissible conventions.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting authorities, under certain conditions, to retrospectively outsource further works if this need arises after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for the correct determination of the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, under the applicable regulations, contracting entities can freely designate one of the open procurement procedures, namely an open procedure or a narrower procedure.
German regulations in tendering and budgetary law enshrine the primacy of open procedures for both European tenders and national ones, known as public calls for competition.
Open procurement procedures in both countries allow a larger range of bidders to submit a bid and also reduce the risk of corruption because they make it difficult to negotiate prices illegally between bidders.
At the same time, the best results in value for money are achieved.
In the case of large contracts whose projected total price is or exceeds the limits set by the EU, the audited contracting entities have always tendered a European procurement procedure.
In determining the type of procurement procedure, the correct determination of the estimated value of the contract plays a very important role.
In Germany, databases are used to objectively determine the price of public works, in which the values are based on completed construction projects.
This is an objective enough tool.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the area of road construction, but they are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a works contract according to European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish preliminary information and notice of the initiation of a procurement procedure, in order to strengthen competition by allowing interested parties to prepare in a timely manner for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
When selecting candidates to participate in a narrower procurement procedure, there is a greater risk of manipulation.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee real competition.
The selection criteria must be objective, non-discriminatory and published well in advance.
Beyond EU requirements, contracting authorities in both countries should, as often as possible, rotate candidates who call for participation in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, there are partly very detailed requirements to prevent the risks of manipulation.
In some cases, the audited contracting entities did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (point 4.4).
In both countries, contracting entities must ensure during the procurement procedure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between those candidates and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must, as far as possible, be prevented in order to avoid restricting competition in the form of unlawful agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the audited German contracting entities did not meet the requirement in several cases (paragraph 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality requirements are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (point 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of bids is procedurally regulated in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more complex, but it has not always been respected by the audited contracting authorities.
For example, contracting entities forwarded their bids to third parties for consultation without their prior assessment, without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
The offers were thus insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulations (point 4.7).
Participants must be concerned about the intended award of the contract for legal protection enterprises are to be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule also is designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The procurement procedure documentation was not always complete or entirely credible for the audited contracting entities in both countries.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level have not always been properly complied with in the minutes of the procedure.
In addition, some identified deficiencies reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the dossier.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly fulfilled their reporting obligations (point 4.9).
Summary of the guiding principles of European procurement law and corruption prevention
European Award law
In order to ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union have adopted, inter alia, a Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts in order to ensure the free movement of goods and services and the Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (Right Procedures Directive), the two Directives being transposed into national law.
The EU's general procurement principles include in particular: a requirement for a competitive environment; a requirement for equal treatment or non-discrimination; a requirement for transparency.
EU procurement principles include the Directive on the Coordination of Public Procurement Procedures, in addition, includes provisions relating to construction projects with a total estimated value of more than €5 million.
These provisions affect: the content of the procurement documents; the type of procurement procedure used; the publication of certain information before, during and after the end of the procedure; compliance with the deadline for receipt of tenders; the qualification requirements that may be required of tenderers; the criteria for selecting the most appropriate tender.
The legal protection of candidates is governed by the Review Procedures Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of contract law, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed in accordance with the review procedures.
Both countries transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Prevention of corruption
Importance of corruption prevention
In none of the two countries, there is no legal definition of what corruption actually means.
In general, corruption is seen as an abuse of a personal (confidential) position for one's own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such misuse is encouraged by those who benefit.
Corruption can be a barrier to competition and can cause serious economic losses.
It is estimated that the total damage related to corruption amounts to 5% of the world's economic output.
For the EU area, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic performance.
Where a contracting authority is involved, conventions restricting competition or allowing dishonest conduct may weaken confidence in the integrity, ability and overall work of the public administration.
Therefore, the prevention of corruption is an essential task for contracting authorities.
Both practice and procedures must be controlled, in particular, in areas particularly susceptible to corruption, in order to take adequate measures to prevent it.
Adopting preventive measures and carrying out effective controls can substantially reduce the susceptibility of the decision-making process to corruption.
As a rule, the award of works contracts is considered to be particularly susceptible to corruption.
Measures on the prevention of corruption
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, which mainly concerns active and passive bribery, acceptance and offering significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual with recommendations on the prevention of corruption in federal administration and according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process beyond risk analysis: priority of open procurement; principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; separation of planning, procurement and accounting phases; accurate and complete documentation of the procurement procedure.
Moreover, other measures having preventive influence against corruption have become part of the right to tender.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry of Information Technology introduced a system to monitor the procurement of federal roads.
Using this system, data such as estimated costs, the total value of the contract and the involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analysed in order to identify any irregularities.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is being prosecuted under criminal law, and the level of labour or civil law is also possible.
The statutory requirement for transparency, equal treatment and prohibition of any discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of corruption prevention.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were established by the government in the "Government Strategy to Fight Corruption for the period 2011 to 2012" which was updated for 2013 and 2014.
Part of these measures were incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and the follow-up implementing regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: lowering the limits for small-scale contracts; the obligation of prior notice by the contracting authority, including the justification of the contract; the prohibition on limiting the number of tenderers in a narrower procedure and the abolition of the general possibility to limit the number of tenderers by drawing lots; the obligation to publish contracts including annexes; the obligation to publish actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of checks relating to subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Procurement documentation
The tender documents must contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their bids in due form.
In particular, the documentation must allow the bidding price to be processed.
For this to be possible, candidates must not be exposed to risks they could not count on.
The tender documents contain, in particular, specifications, as well as the requirements for proving the qualifications of the tenderers and the criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
In particular, European law contains requirements relating to technical specifications.
The specifications must limit competition to the least extent possible and all candidates must have equal access to them.
The specification shall be neutral.
Requirements for specific products are permissible on a very limited scale in the tender documents.
The basic principle of preventing corruption is that the tender documents do not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, the documentation should avoid confusion or inaccurate data that create room for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that the specifications are generally stated without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specifications shall specify the required supplies and services and detail the number of units of work and supply.
In normal cases, prices should be quoted according to works or supply units (e.g. in CZK/m or in CZK/item).
Defining the required work in terms of their functionality is only permissible in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that the specifications need to be developed in full and unambiguous way.
They must contain exact quantities and must not include works and supplies, the necessity of which is not entirely clear.
Reimbursement in the form of lump sums or according to time worked must be avoided where possible.
These parts of the tender documents must exclude speculation and manipulation, based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must allow each tenderer to prepare their offer with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender must only be made public when the tender documents are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for subpoena to award construction contracts in parts.
A contracting authority may divide the contract into several separate parts if the nature of the subject-matter of such a contract so permits.
In Germany, the application of the rule is required to divide construction work into parts, either quantitatively (partial deliveries) or according to specific aspects (specialised part of the contract).
This allows participation in competition to small and medium-sized entities, increases the number of potential bidders and makes price fixing more difficult.
This type of input is usually considered to be the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, the tender documents must include evaluation criteria in national and European procurement procedures.
This should inform the tenderer comprehensively and exhaustively of the criteria by which its tender will be assessed.
In Germany, the requirement to indicate the evaluation criteria in the notice applies only to European calls for tenders.
In national procurement procedures, it is necessary to include evaluation criteria only in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
If the tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, German regulations require the exclusion from it of any information that would help identify specific interested firms, architects or designers.
The reason is to prevent individual construction firms from obtaining any additional information or other inadmissible competitive advantages without the satisfaction of the contracting authority through contacts with these independent experts.
This is also why independent experts are not allowed to publish tender documents and allow them to be accessed for vetting.
The contracting authority is the only contact point for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, there is almost always a need for additional work or modification of existing works after the conclusion of the original contract.
The legislation of both countries allows a multi-works contract to be awarded to the same contractor within a certain limit, without the need to publish and carry out a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional works under both European and German legislation may not exceed 50% of the original value of the contract.
In addition, additional work must be necessary and must be in the nature of a specific component of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied to award additional works.
This may risk a greater time delay in the execution of the contract and the possible integration of the new entity into the project already running.
Where a multi-works contract is awarded to the supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision which prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified works or multi-works.
Calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to extra work, there may be less work during construction, i.e. works that were not needed.
In the Czech Republic, less work and extra work cannot be compensated for each other.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In rare cases, the specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but these have been replaced in the meantime by EU standards.
In some cases, the specifications contained data requiring a trade mark, e.g. for a system of resilience of public inscriptions, for preventing water penetration or for noise-cancelling, although this was not needed.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications broken down into items
Even in a single case of scrutiny of procurement procedures, the BRH did not record non-compliance with the general requirements for the invitation to tender for supplies and services according to the quantitative specifications divided into items.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, the tender documents were fully drawn up before the call for tenders was announced.
However, in various cases, documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure when changes in the items or timetable had to be taken into account.
In rare cases, it contained the tender documents for the work and supplies, the need for which was uncertain.
One tender dossier for demolition work contained almost exclusively such items.
Contract splitting and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for not dividing deliveries into parts has not been demonstrated.
For the evaluation criteria for tenders prior to the award of the contract, BRH found no shortcomings.
Include the names of authorised independent experts
Many of the documents featured interested authorized experts.
Names were listed on the opening pages of documents or on plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact the explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the tendering procedure.
For building projects, these cases were found mainly in the case of tender documents, where self-employed professionals were responsible for processing the specifications.
The contracting authorities said they did not have sufficient staffing capacity to scrutinize the tender documents in detail.
SAO
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house of 12 flats, the reference was directly to specific manufacturers and brands of products.
The contracting authority did not allow the use of other similar types of products nor justified why it required the delivery of specific products.
For the construction of a college facility, the tender documents included references to specific trading firms and specific product labels.
Overview of specifications and sub-species
For the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the tender documents the quantity and type of required works, supplies or services in the statement of assessment and set only the enclosed space in cubic metres for each building as a specific unit.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of the ring road, the tender documents for the narrower procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation triggered by an extension of the scope of the contract and a change in the technical conditions.
For the construction of social objects, the tender documentation had defects that forced the creation of additional works (there was no precise focus, a sufficient construction-technical survey was not carried out, drawings were missing).
The tender documentation for the construction of a large library featured interior elements only in descriptive terms, without specifying the qualitative requirements for products.
The delivery of the interior furnishings of the building was provided by subcontractors, who purchased some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for awarding the contract
There is no obligation to award a contract in parts in the Czech Republic.
An analysis of three large road constructions carried out in the construction of the ring road showed that when they were commissioned in open driving in parts (after each other after selected buildings), savings of up to 790 million could be achieved. CZK from the achieved public procurement price 15,508 mil. CZK.
For example, when awarding six building objects as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 62.5 million could be achieved.
CZK, i.e. 25% of the contracted price and when awarding 20 bridges as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 287.8 million could be achieved. CZK, i.e. also 25%.
The contracting authority, when constructing roads in the tender documents, laid down qualification criteria that limited the participation of small contractors in the procurement procedure.
In the tender conditions of the open procedure for the contractor for one construction of the motorway construction, the contracting authority requested, among other things, the contractor's practice of implementing at least three road constructions with a financial volume of more than EUR 700 million each. CZK, completed or operational over the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completing buildings over CZK 700 million. CZK in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of another highway construction in an open procedure included six separate works for which the contracting authority had requested a minimum annual volume of construction work in the last three years of EUR 12 billion.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. € 2.5 billion out of € 3.7 billion) in the period of 2008–2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or either individually in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of 13.2 billion CZK, there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that limiting market potential could lead to a large increase in costs.
Area of multi-works
In the construction of the ring road, the contracting authority did not enter into a written contract for the supply of additional works of construction in two cases, and the extra work did not enter into any of the procedures set out in the Act.
The value of pre-approved additional works in one case was EUR 1 591.4 million. CZK without VAT (an increase of 21%) and in the second case 4 135.8 mil. CZK without VAT (increase of 91%).
The reason for the extra work was mainly the ambiguous definition of the quantity and type of work required.
For the construction of a university facility, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documents certain works and supplies, the necessity of which it was already aware of when awarding the contract.
For these works and deliveries, he concluded amendments to the contract for the work with the contractor of the construction with an increase in the price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional works exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Justice Palace, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a procurement procedure.
During construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million. CZK.
The unit prices of additional works determined without competition were significantly higher than the comparable works carried out by the same supplier.
Other findings
The SAO found on a selected sample of buildings that the average construction costs for the construction of one kilometre of the four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic are 355.0 million. CZK.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometre section of the four-lane motorway is CZK 260.0 million. CZK.
This comparison suggests that the acquisition of a motorway in the Czech Republic is more expensive than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions on tender documents are almost identical in both countries and are in line with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and prevention of corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow interested parties to calculate the costs and prepare their bids.
In addition, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to outsource additional work.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on tender documents are complemented by other rules designed to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and carrying out manipulation.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, there is higher transparency achieved by publishing award criteria.
In both countries, specification requirements are mostly met.
However, in some cases, deficiencies have been identified which could lead to a reduction in competition.
At the same time, the rule in Germany requiring the division of bulky construction projects into parts seems appropriate for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, awarding contracts for large-scale projects without partial procurement or division into large parts can limit the circle of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
The strict requirement for references of bidders, which is applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of bidders for road construction work, is the fact that public contracts are awarded only to a small circle of bidders, and small and medium-sized companies thus have no chance of competing.
A limited number of applicants also increases the risk of fixing prices and other illegal conventions.
As regards the award of additional contracts for additional work or modified work and their reimbursement, regulatory approaches are different in both countries.
If the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50% of the value of the original contract, additional works may be awarded without a procurement procedure and its notification.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20%.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by the sponsor.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the 20 % limit is exceeded, additional work must be awarded on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
By dividing the work into parts, it is possible to avoid restricting competition as well as the subsequent dire consequences.
Identification of the type of procurement procedure with respect to EU limits
By determining the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the circle of potential contractors who will be admitted to the competition.
A procuring entity may keep the range of potential suppliers unlimited or may limit the range according to the criteria entered.
In the case of major project work whose value is or exceeds the limits set by the EU, a European procurement procedure must be conducted.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
In particular, when choosing the type of procedure, the contracting authority must comply with the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimate of the total cost of the contract for the required works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million (€125 million). CZK), it is always required to issue an open or restricted procurement procedure at European level.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is permitted.
If a construction project reaches an EU limit, the contracting authority may deviate to a limited extent from the European procurement procedure by issuing a national procedure where the total value of the relevant part is below €1.0 million (€25 million). CZK) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the value of the estimate of the entire contract.
Dividing a construction project or intentionally estimating its costs too low with the intention of avoiding a fundamental obligation to call a European procurement procedure is not permissible.
Choosing the type of proceedings is very important for preventing corruption, as restricting competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, an important part of the procurement documents is a detailed justification for the type of procedure chosen, in particular in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU's legal requirements for procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and narrower procedures as well as for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and narrower procedures can always be chosen, while other types of proceedings are only allowed if the conditions laid down by law are met.
Unlike the Czech regulation, the relevant German regulation of budgetary law and the principles of prevention of corruption prefers open procedures or a public call for participation in the proceedings.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted procedures, are permitted only exceptionally, where permitted by legislation.
Such exceptions must be justified and documented.
The principle of open management preference is intended to ensure that the restriction of competition is prevented to the maximum extent and that the opportunity for manipulation (previously selected specific candidates) is limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is at the heart of corruption prevention.
The correct estimate of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
When estimating the costs of building projects, contracting entities in Germany can rely on budgetary and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These were compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for building types, building units, construction parts and partial construction works.
Based on such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the value of the contract of the planned project or works.
Only a few building administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be expanded at national level.
Contracting entities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable objects to determine the expected costs of public contracts for ground construction.
In many cases, cost determination is based on an estimate by the project-funded entity or based on the value of the resources available to that body.
The standardisation measures are still at an early stage.
Based on the anti-corruption programme for the period 2011-2012, the relevant ministries are to draw up a database of standardised commodities prices.
The application of the price system for determining the expected price of the contract is determined by a new regulation ordering the determination of the subject-matter of the contract in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly work, materials and products and other data to calculate the necessary costs and to set unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued prescriptive prices for transport constructions for their control, which are regularly updated.
The database is designed to determine the price of a project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of construction work preparation.
Main BRH control findings
Selection of procurement procedure
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts in an open procedure or in the form of a public call for participation and thus complied with the requirements of budgetary and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of public tenders in the total volume of federal construction projects in the field of building construction was 59.5%.
In road construction, the share was 94.8%.
BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public competition was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of blanket control, BRH found, on the basis of information collected from more than 16,000 proceedings, that limited management has a clear disadvantage compared to public proceedings and a public challenge, and limited procedures also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, tenders in restricted procedures and negotiated procedures were between 6% and 10% higher than in public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Land Inspectorate confirmed the differences in value for money raised in public and limited management.
A study by the Ministry of Construction even found that prices in restricted procedures were twice as high as those in public proceedings.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects whose total value of the contract was estimated at more than €5 million (€125 million). CZK), audited contracting authorities generally prepared tender plans specifying which works would be awarded in the European procurement procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the procurement plans, the share of works awarded under European rules always exceeded 80% of the total work.
There has been no case of circumvention of the requirement for European management in the form of an exorbitantly low cost estimate.
However, in some cases the minimum proportion for pan-European proceedings has not been achieved as a result of the calculation error.
In the course of the work, some contracting authorities illegally changed the procurement plan.
They did not justify these changes in any way and did not show that the minimum share of European management was subsequently respected.
As a rule, the share of European management by project accounts was lower than the share set out in the procurement plan.
If the expected values of construction project contracts exceeded European limits, contracting entities did not apply the European procedure to all individual sub-parts values exceeding €1 million (€25 million). CZK).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure, without giving good reasons for the need for a derogation from the requirement.
SAO
Selection of procurement procedure type
In the case of several large construction projects (e.g. the construction of a road section, equipment for a large sports event and the construction of flats), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, although these partial orders were of the same or similar nature.
The division resulted in a reduction in the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded in a less demanding regime or even outside the regime of the law.
Methods of calculating the expected price
Price standards applicable to road construction projects have not yet been an effective instrument for setting the expected price.
They were based on the prices set out in the completed procedures, but they were burdened with both wastefulness and excessive profit for suppliers.
The SAO found that the actual prices for the three parts of the ring road were about 37% lower than these price standards.
The Ministry of Transport is to update standards in relation not only to past prices, but also to other comparable markets and price developments.
For land buildings, the estimated price was based on the budget of the project carried out by the architects or on the investor's estimate.
The actual price then varied considerably from the expected price.
The estimated cost of building a large library was twice as high compared to the contract price, but increased by CZK 54 million during the implementation. CZK, i.e. 27%.
Similarly, for the construction of the depository of this library, the contract price was 33 % lower than the expected price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the expected price and increased further during the implementation.
For the construction of a large library, it was 400 miles. CZK, i.e. 22%. For the construction of the Palace of Justice, the actual price during the construction process increased disproportionately (by 139%) compared to the increase in functional areas (only by 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of type of procurement procedure and on respect for European limits in their national legislation, so the respective national adjustments of the two countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public procedures in Germany is stricter than the European arrangements and the current national regulation in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a narrower procurement procedure, a public procedure or a public call for participation gives opportunities for more competitors to participate in a procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of bids that guarantee the best value effect for money.
Prevention of corruption is significantly strengthened as the circle of candidates is not limited by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which thus gives room for manipulation and fixing prices among bidders.
The studies confirmed the advantages of the German provision on the preference of open procedures or public calls for participation.
The Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to award contracts in an open or narrower procedure.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a prohibition on contracting authorities to limit the number of tenderers in narrower procedures.
Closer proceedings have now come very close to open proceedings.
In Germany, the contracting authority largely fulfils the requirement of a preference for an open procedure or a public call for competition, while in the Czech Republic some projects were divided in violation of the law, in order to circumvent the requirement to choose the relevant type of procurement procedure.
However, for some projects carried out in Germany, European limits were not respected, resulting in a minimum share of pan-European procedures.
A satisfactory system has not yet been introduced in the Czech Republic to facilitate the correct calculation of expected costs of construction work and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting entities should ensure, through more extensive checks, that the requirements for the selection of the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are met, or should create the conditions necessary to allow for the correct calculation of expected costs.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation
Where contracts are awarded under the European procedure, the contracting authority must publish a procurement report.
Preliminary information and procurement announcements are an important basis for European competition.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
Through prior notifications, the contracting authority must provide the basic specifications of the projects envisaged.
Preliminary notifications are required for projects where the estimate of total costs is equal to or greater than the European limit and when the contracting authority wishes to shorten the deadline for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority is obliged to publish a procurement notice with detailed information for all public contracts that require a European procedure.
The information is binding on the contracting authority after publication.
Among other things, the notification shall include specifications, time limits for the procedure and for the completion of the contract, specific requirements and selection criteria and their weightings.
To describe the subject-matter of the contract, a common dictionary must be used for public contracts.
Preliminary information and contract notices are published in the EU in the EU in the Official Journal of the EU and on the profile of the contracting authority.
In order to avoid discrimination against foreign candidates, notifications at national level are not to contain any additional information and are not to be published before the text of the EU Publications Office's notification is sent.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in a pre-notification may violate competition or even ease manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU provisions on preliminary information and procurement notices in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, preliminary information and notifications must also be published in the case of contracts for which a European procedure is not required.
Procurement procedures may not be initiated until one month after the prior notification has been sent.
The publication of the provisional information may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, preliminary information is not required in cases where a European procedure is not necessary.
In the case of national procedures, the public appeal must be published on the Federal Government's website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted procedures without competition from participants, a notice must be published where the estimated price of the contract is equal to or greater than €25,000 ($625,000).
Preliminary notifications must explain all planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small contracts in Germany.
Dividing projects into smaller parts is not as common in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Prior notification
Contracting entities published a pre-notice notice on projects that included the work to be commissioned in the European proceedings.
In some open proceedings, they shortened the deadline, although the number of days between the sending of the prior notification and the sending of the contract notice was less than 52 days.
In the case of other summary procedures, the annual periods applicable to the provisional information expired and should have been renewed.
In many cases, the prior notification did not indicate the anticipated division of the works into multiple parts.
Notice of initiation of a procurement procedure
Some notifications contained codes from the common dictionary that deviated from the pre-notification content or did not specify precisely the work to be carried out.
In rare cases, notifications at national level included more information, e.g. on delivery dates, or were published before the notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority hired an external company to publish a national notice.
It published a notice on its platform, the use of which has been charged.
But the contracting authority did not check if the company had to comply with the request to send a further notice to a free online government portal.
The sample revealed that only a third of the notifications were actually published on the government portal.
SAO
Notice of initiation of a procurement procedure
When constructing a wastewater treatment plant, the contracting authority did not set out in the notification of the shortlisted procurement procedure for the construction contractor the criteria according to which it would reduce the large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure.
The method of selection (reduction) of the tenders for the evaluation was not able to be substantiated by the contracting authority.
Reducing the number of bids was made possible by previous legislation.
In the construction of the road circuit, the contracting authority did not specify in the notice of initiation of a narrower procedure or in the qualification documentation of the values by which it would assess the degree of fulfilment of the qualification criteria or the number of bidders to be invited.
The contracting authority assessed the fulfilment of the qualification in such a way that all six candidates in the short term complied with it, but excluded one of them, saying that it fulfilled only in the last sixth place.
The contracting authority's action was not transparent as it did not disclose in advance information on what the qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Disclosure of calls
For building modifications in former military installations, the contracting authority did not disclose, in violation of the law, a written invitation to submit tenders.
Conclusions and recommendations / summary
The two-stage pre-information information process and the publication of the notice of initiation is an important tool for meeting the transparency and strengthening of the competitive environment applied in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish preliminary information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and by strengthening this obligation in Germany in cases of restricted procedures, both countries increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
Contrary to the open procedure, limited procurement procedures do not allow participation to all potential bidders who would be interested.
In restricted procedures, the contracting authority shall first select potential suppliers to be asked to submit a tender.
Requirements of European law and the prevention of corruption
EU provisions allow the number of candidates in European restricted procedures to be capped.
However, a restricted procedure requires at least five tenderers to be invited to submit a tender and, in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication, at least three potential bidders must be invited.
In any event, the number of candidates invited must be broad enough to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria must be objective and non-discriminatory and published in advance.
The selection of potential candidates to be invited is very susceptible to manipulation, e.g. preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders may ease their collusion.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the proceedings.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent manipulation and ensure that it has effective control to ensure that this procedure is transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The provisions for selecting candidates are similar in both countries.
